The beautiful flat at 1004 McAllister
street was deserted last night, save for
the presence of Mrs. Muir, the mother
of Mrs. Pittman. Mrs. Muir was ter
ribly shocked when she learned of the
death of Captain Wild, and at first re
fused to believe that he could have
taken his life. She said her daughter
had brought his picture to her while
she was living in Canada and asked
her what she thought of him. Mrs.
Muir replied that while she liked his
appearance as a man, she did not think
he tvould make a good husband. He
looked too severe. Mrs. Muir said Mrs.
Plttman was in Oakland and would not
be home until some time to-day.
The last seen of Captain Wild by his
army friends in this city was shortly
before midnight Wednesday, when in
company with a number of other offi
cers he was enjoying the Jinks that fol
lowed the meeting of the Military
Order of the Carabao at the Occidental
Hotel. If he was contemplating suicide
then his manner did not in any way
suggest the fact to his friends, who left
the hotel with him when the meeting
adjourned. On Montgomery street the
captain bade them good-n!ght and in
dicated that he purposed to return to
the island. This he evidently did some
time before morning. The first intima
tion his friends on this side of the bay
received of his death was late yester
day afternoon, when a telephone mes-"
sage from the island conveyed the in
formation that his body had been dis
covered, with a pistol lying by its side.
SOLDIER WITH MESSAGE.
A grand piano, a pianola, gorgeous
tapestries and everything that the en
vironment of wealth could give decor
ates the dainty flat. Some months
ago It Is reported that the captain
commenced to feel that he could not
keep up the strain and often in the
presence of his friends in the Bohe
mian Club he was wont to assert that
without money a man should not live.
While he assumed a Jovial air at the
club, it was apparent to all his friends,
and they were legion, that he was la
boring under a mental strain.
MRS. PITTMAN ABSENT.
Captain Wild's death has caused a
great sensation in army circles. From
what can be learned and from report*
undoubtedly authentic, it appears in
a direct way that a woman was at
the bottom and the cause of his
death. . Wh en Captain Wild came
home from the Philippines two years
ago he brought with him $4000.
Shortly after his arrival here he be
came acquainted with Mrs. Evelyn
Plttman. From that time until now
she has resided In a luxurious flat at
1004 McAllister street- Every acces
sory that elegance and refinement
could suggest Is there.

LIAOYANG, June 8. â The Japanese on June 6, according to Chinese reports, made several sustained and stubborn attacks upon Port Arthur simultaneously by land and sea. They were repulsed with severe loss. There are rumors
that the Vladivostok squadron has effected a junction with the Port Arthur fleet ; that a naval battle took place, and that the Japanese lost four large ships.
VICTOR, Colo., June 8. â A pitched battle between the military and union. miners was fought at Dunnville, the new mining- camp thirteen miles out of Victor, shortly after 3 o'clock this afternoon. John Carley, a union miner, was
killed. The troops returned to Victor at 8 o'clock to-night, bringing with them fourteen captives. Later there was another fight between a squad of soldiers and two intrenched miners; the latter were captured.

Captain Wild was a native of New
York State, 41 years of age and un
married. He entered the army as a
private soldier In 1887, and ten years
later he was appointed from the ranks
to the position of first lieutenant. In
1S99 be was made a captain and was
eent to the Philippines with the Thir
teenth Infantry. He returned here
about two years ago, and later
was assigned to duty as regi
mental commissary at Fort McDowell,
Angel Island. Among the army folk
he was a very popular officer, and his
tragic death. was the last thing expect
ed by his many friends at the various
posts.

Dunnville sprang into prominence
â¢last week, when it was reported that

been discovered, and there was an Im

men, who declared that no others

trolled the party.

SEATTLE, June 8. â J. B. Allison
and Edgar Marshall, two of the most
desperate prisoners In the County
Jail, made an attempt to escape this
morning.- An exciting battle was
fought between the deputies and the
prisoners, the tide being turned in fa
vor of the officials by the trusties.
Marshall and Allison sawed the bars
of the. steel cage in which they wer*
confined and when Deputy Sheriff
Hippe was superintending the removal
of garbage from the cage to the kitch
en the desperate men made a break
for liberty. The door of the room In
which the cage is located was open.
It leads to the Jailer's office, and from
the office there is a passage to the
kitchen, where a door leads to the
outside world.-

NORFOLK. June 8.â The United
States authorities are worried over the
suspicious actions of the Norwegian
steamship Fortuna, which sailed from
this port several days ago for "Cork
direct." It is reported the Fortuna
was anchored in Princess Bay, near
Perth Amboy, and finally sailed away
in a mist with what appeared to be a
submarine boat lashed on her deck
and which gave her a heavy list to
port.
Yesterday several men visited the
custom-house, secured all the Infor
mation, about her sailing and clearing
papers and hastily embarked on a
steamship for Old Point. There they
left for sea on a "yacht," supposed to
have been a revenue cutter.

Bout on Board.

CHICAGO, June S.â James Cantant
of Melrose Park is searching for his
wife, his two little girls and his sav
ings, amounting to J1000., He declares
that Charles Yuknewich is responsible
for the disappearance of "wife, children
and money. He believeg they have
gone to San Francisco and the local
police have asked the California au
thorities to look out for them.
. "I am particularly desirous of find
ing the children and the $1000. As for
the others, I am not so particular,"
said Cantant, when he told" of the dis-"
appearance to the police.
According to Cantant, Yuknewich
went to Melrose Park some weeks ago
and. ehortly after his arrival, became
acquainted with Cantant's wife. The
man called at his house many times,
but he had not the slightest idea that
his wife had formed an attachment for
Yuknewich. The news was first bro
ken to him when he hied to his home
yesterday at noon and found no lunch
eon i prepared. Me then searched the
house for his wife and children, but
they*were missing. His hunt for the
money was as fruitless as was his
search for his family. He Immediately
told the police of the disappearance.
The children are girls and are 3 and

Special Dispatch to The Call.
ST. PETERSBURG. June 8.â The
reports of the sorties from and at
tacks on Port Arthur coming In from
foreign sources arouse the liveliest
interest here. The general staff is be
coming more reticent and the public
is convinced that an important battle,
which may decide the fate of the cam
paign, is impending. The outpost en
gagements between the main armies
of Generals Kuropatkin and Kuroki
have suddenly ceased. Such a lull fre
quently occurs after two armies have
been for some- time in touch and just
before they are ready to strike. Ku
roki has not yet moved, according to
the latest advices, but is drawing in
his skirmishers, as if preparing to do
so. Kuropatkin has elaborately forti
fied his position at Liaoyang.
Kuropatkln and Kuroki Soon to Meet
. In Decisive Combat.
GREAT BATTLE IS XEARIXG.
No attention is paid in London to re
mors that the storming of the fort
resses has already begun, because It Is
known that at the battle of Klnchou
General Oku had exausted his ammu
nition and an insufficient period has
elapsed duftng which the stores of am
munition pould be renewed and siege
guns brought into position. It is con
sidered likely that Admiral Togo is not
averse to Port Arthur wasting its am
munition in constant encounters with
the gunboats. This may explain the
rumored bombardments^ while the fir
ing on the land side may be due to pre
liminary operations having for their
purpose driving In the Russian forces.
X.ONDON, June 8.â The sudden in
terruption of the cable between Korea
and Japan is considered significant of
the imminence of important operations
at Port Arthur, the Japanese having
taken precautions, as usual, to 'cut the
means of communication with the out
side world and thus enable their naval
and military forces to work with ab
solute secrecy.
Two regiments of Ural Cossacks, the
pride and hppe of the Russian cavalry,
have been ordered to the front.
-Transports, twenty-five in number,
with full munitions and store, are
awaiting the clearance, of mines from
TaUenwan. Â¦
Here military critics are thoroughly
nonplused at the situation and all
agree that no immediate move will be
made toward the rescue of Port Ar
thur and that the Japaneese retreat is
in the nature of a feint.

Special Cable to The Call and Xew York Her
ald. Copyright. 1904, by the New York

ST. PETERSBURG.- June 8. â Over
and over agrain I have- heard anger
and ridicule expressed at the fantastic
reports sent from here suggesting that
General Kuropatkin'was being forced
Into a plan of campaign against his
judgment. On all sides these are de
scribed as totally untrue and invented
by those who wish to throw discredit
upon Russia.
A thousand carts are being em
ployed in removing stores from Xew
chwang to a point behind Mukden.
When this is completed he will deliver
a crushing blow to the enemy.
At one moment he contemplated
sacrificing Newchwang, but the value
of the war material there and the
shortage of the same at his head
quarters owing to the amount rushed
into Port Arthur at the last moment
made it of vital importance to hold
the place until the stores were saved.
This will explain much of Kuropat
kin's tactics that have hitherto been
somewhat inexplicable.
Kuroki's advance forces have made
several attempts to creep around the
Russian flank, but have been invari
ably repelled by the Cossacks. Simul
taneously cruisers and gunboats have
been bombarding the Kwantung coast.

Four delegates to the national con
ventions from the Philippines arrived
yesterday on the liner Coptic. Two of

of First Instance, one of them is a Ma
nila merchant, and the other is Deputy
Collector of Customs at Manila. , None
of the dqjegates is sure of a seat at the
convention. This is the first time the
Philippines have been in line for repre
sentation and the question of recogniz
ing the delegates will be one of the
earliest problems before the commit
tees on credentials. \
Judge A. C. Carson is a delegate to
the Democratic convention and Judge
Trent has been sent to represent hla
constituents at the Republican . gather
ing.-^iJep'uty Collector J. S. Stanley
and J. 4 M v Switzer will also, if the con
vention.decldes to receive. "them, attend
the Chicago meeting. ,T. C. Reiser of
the Manila customs service, comes as
an. alternate to the Republican conven
tion, "and Â¦ also;, as ' a delegate to the
Elks' annual gathering.
_Judge H. C. Bates, also of the. Court
of First Instance,' was' another passen
ger on, the CoDtic.. He is home on a
leave of absence. , . . s
/* According to the judicial trio peace
is a permanent institution in the Phil
ippines. The recent outbreak in Min
danao,' they, say, .while, serious in itself,

way indicative of any general feeling
of unrest.

Two young ladies, natives of, India,
in search of education as they expect
to find it in America, arrived here yes
terday from the. Orient on 'the liner
Coptic. They are the Misses Ethel and
Dora Maya Das and are* daughters of
a. high caste East Indian, who holds

Miss Ethel is somewhat of a dazzling
beauty and both girls are highly edu
cated. They are traveling under the
chaperonage of Rev. F. ' S. Hatch, M.
A., who is general secretary of the
Christian Endeavor. Society in India.
The reverend chaperon has been in the
Far East. for three years and during
that time has visited branches of the
Christian Endeavor Society in India,
China and Japan, traveling, in so do
ing, more than 40,000 miles..
Commissioner North said that Dr.
Gardiner had acted within the line of
I was on duty at the gangplank when Mrs.
Riggs came down with her brother and her
n*u>hew. Both the men had the proper papers,
signed by Honolulu officials, which entitled
thorn to land, but ths lady had no papors
such as are required by law. or any papers
at all. In such matters the law leaves the
official without an}' discretion, and it dbes not
exempt any persons of the Chinese race from
Its requirements, no matter whether they are
married to Americans or not. The momeiV
that the steamship company notifies us that
Chinese or descendants of Chinese are on
board it la our duty to stop them until they
prove that they -are entitled to land. If we
did not do It we would be liable to prosecution.
Every courtesy was shown the lady and her
statement was taken, as required by the law.
I found her to be a lady or refinement and
culture.
The lady arrived with Toney C.
Afong and Chun Wing Sen Afong, her
brother and nephew, who came from.
Shanghai by the steamship Siberia
and stopped over at Honolulu to ac
company their sister hither. Dr. John
Endicott Gardiner, a Chinese inspec
tor, was at the gangplank and stopped
the party. The brother and nephew
exhibited papers, signed by the proper
officials at Honolulu, certifying that
they were American citizens under the
act annexing Hawaif to the United
States. Immediately upon the produc
tion of. the papers they were allowed
to land. Mrs. Carrie Riggs, however,
had no papers and Dr. Gardiner, who
is one of the most courteous officials
in the Federal service, informed her
that under the law and his instruc
tions he had no discretion, but would
have to report her arrival to his su
perior officer..
Mrs. Riggs was inclined to be in
dignant, but after the situation had
been . explained, she acknowledged
that, unpleasant as the episode had
proved to her, the inspector was sim r
ply doing his duty and in the least of
fensive way possible. Dr. Gardiner at
once communicated with Charles Me
han. Inspector in charge of the Chi
nese bureau, and Mr. Mehan directed
that the usual statement should be
taken from her as to her identity, age,
birthplace, etc. This was done and
when Immigrant Commissioner North
arrived at his office shortly after 9
a. m. Mr. Mehan laid the facts before
him, whereupon the Commissioner or?
dered that she be landed without fur
ther ceremony. An inspector was sent
to the steamer with the order and the
lady was released at once. /The steam
er arrived at the wharf at 7:45 a. m.
and Mrs. Riggs went ashore at
10:46 a. m.
Dr. Gardiner made the following
statement regarding the affair:
lire. Carrie Riggs, a young and
talented member of the Afong family,
was detained for three hours on board
the steamship Coptic yesterday-fore
noon by the exigencies of the Chinese
exclusion act.

Warner, obtained a warrant for her

mother. Hockenberry has not yet been
found.

MEYER'S STORY OF TRAGEDY.
According to Meyer, who says he
witnessed the scene, 'the shooting of
Young appeared to be accidental. Mrs.
Patterson was struggling with Young,
who had a revolver in his right hand
and his right arm was around her
neck. To Meyer it appeared as though
Mrs. Patterson was trying to push
Young away from her. or, perhaps, to
take the revolver away from him.
According to the policeman who first
reached the cab Mrs. Patterson sat at
th<* left of Young.
Meyer says there was another wit
ness to the tragedy. This was a man
fibout five fert eight inches tall, at

woman in the cab.
About this witness all manner of

H. A. Katz. The directory docs not

The Merchants' Exchange has under
taken the management of another
Fourth of July celebration for Oakland.
The programme is to be the most am
bitious attempted for several years.
There will be a parade in the morning,
literary exercises immediately follow
ing, athletic exercises on the shore of
Lake Merritt and boat races on the
lake in the afternoon,, with fireworks
in the evening.
The Merchants' Exchange has under
taken to raise $S.V)O this year, which is
$1500 more than has ever been raised
before. There is a small sum in the
treasury of the Merchants' Exchange
to the credit of the Fourth of July
fund, and committees are now at work
trying to raise the additional sum nec
essary. Already more than $2000 has
been subscribed.
Former Congressman Julius Kahn
will deliver the oration at ' the after
noon literary exercises. The committee
has not yet decided whether they will
secure one of the local theaters for the
literary programme or erect a tempo
rary stand on the banks of Lake Mer
ritt.

Whatever else Mrs. Hearst does, she
will not withdraw her support from
the anthropological department that
she has so liberally patronized. A
persistent rumor that Mrs. Hearst con
templated letting that branch of the
university's activities shift for itself
was denied to-day by Edward Clark,
manager of Mra. Hearst's business af
fairs, who defends his employer's re
cent act'on in refusing to give further
aid to some of. the benefactions she
founded.
Mrs. Hearst, so Mr. Clark says, will
continue to sive her support to the
anthropological department. Her
gifts to assist the work of searching
for the hidden relics of past ages have
amounted to at least $50,000 a year
for several vears. Her agents have
delved beneath the earth and among
the archives of foreign lands until
now . the University of California
boasts of one of the most vamable
collections in the world.
"Mrs. Hearat will continue to sup
port the archaeological department,"
said Manager Clark at his office in
San Francisco th,is morning. "It ia
true that the contracts with some of
the scientific men ! engaged to carry
on researches will soon expire and
probably will not be renewed. But
the work these men set out to tdo is
about completed and there is no fur
ther need for their services. . Her con
tract with Professor Otto Emerson, for
instance, will expire in "September.
Under Professor Emerson's direction
Mrs. Hearst has spent $30,000 this
last year upon Greek and Egyptian
researches alone. Sh'e has also sup
ported the work of Professor F. \V.
Putnam in America and Professor
Max Uhle in Peru, which have cost
great sums of money.
"It 'seems to me unkind to talk
about Mrs. Hearst withdrawing her
benefactions. I think I can say that
she does not intend to withdraw her
patronage of the university at all. She
has simply cut down on a few things
that did not justify continued expendi
tures of money.- The few she has
abandoned are nothing compared to
the many she is still assisting."
Berkeley Office San Francisco Call,
2148 Centen Street, June 8.

Whether the salaries of the thirty
or forty employes on the infirmary
pay roll can be paid out of the money
now remaining in the fund or at all Is
a question that is Interesting them.
Statutory fees can be paid out of any
available money or can be carried
over into next year, but the wages in
question are not provided _for by stat
ute and if the present month slips
through without their being paid it is
questionable whether they will be
paid at all. The money remaining in
the fund could be used for this pur
pose, but there are outstanding
against this fund bills long overdue,
which were on file before the salaries
accrued and it is believed they must
take precedence in the order of filing.
The butcher and baker, grocer and
coal man have large accounts and the
banks have refused to discount their
claims. The clanu r for money is be
ing heard throughout political circles
and wherever inttuence can be brought
to bear upon the Supervisors to pass
claims. The Grand Jury is still in ses
sion and Ls hampering the movements
of the board. It is believed that as
soon as this official body is out of ex
istence the Supervisors will take some
action looking to the relief of the sit
uation.
Oakland Office San Francisco Call,
1016 Broadway, June S.
The County Infirmary pay roll has
been suspended and no bills have been'
allowed against this fund since May
9. The employes of the County In- |
firmary and Receiving Hospital have
not received their May salaries. The
question is asked how far the Bhortage
will extend. There Is at present to the ,
credit of the infirmary fund the sum
of $4471 58. Against this there are
bills now on file aggregating about
$1.4,000 and a month yet to run be
fore the next year's' money will be
available.
County Assessor Henry P. Dalton
must pay Alameda County ?1999 which
he collected from the city of Oakland
for furnishing the assessment rolls to
the city. In a decision handed down
to-day Judge Melvin decides that Dal
ton has no right to the money. Dalton
charged the city of Oakland $2650 for
preparing the assessment rolls. Of this
amount Judge Melvin found 5651 to be
actual expenses, and that the balance
should have been turned Into the
county treasury.
On the trial of the 'county's suit
against Dalton he claimed he had em
ployed II. H. Miller, one of his deputies,
to do the work and .promised him 5
cents a folio, this being/the amount the
city was willing to pay. Thirteen men
finished the work in ten days and but
two of them were paid, the others testl
fj'ing they never expected anything for
their services.
Judge Melvin said:
The Assessor is required to file with the Au
ditnr "a Â»worn statement showing the persons
to whom the amounts paid to each â¢ â¢ â¢ for
such aspe?Ement rolls." The very purpose of i
requiririK the sworn statement In, detail must
have been the furnishing of data which would
enable those Interested In the matter to deter
mine whether or not the Assessor had re
tained enly the actual cost of the work. There
fore It is clear that the contract with Mr.
Miller was void. That contract being of no
?"fleet, ttie Assessor must pay to the county the
difference between $2650 and the actual cost of
the work. It eeems frcra all the evidence that
a fair estimate of the value of their services,
countlnK overtime, for the days during which i
they worked upon the city's rolls, is $50 for
each man. or aii amount equal to the value of
fifteen days of the ordinary service of these
same men when working the usual number of
hours per day for the Assessor. It make rlo
difference that these men had afrreed to work
for Mr. Miller, their fellow employe, for less
money or for nothing or without any arrange
ment as to compensation. Whatever their re
lations with Mr. Miller might have been, which {
Induced them to reduce or waive comreneatlcn j
when working for him. these relations would
have no bearing upon their claim against fhe
Assessor for the reasonable value of their la
"bor.
From the foregoing it follows that plaintiff
should have judgment for the difference be
tween J2<2Â»'J and $C5l. that Is to say, for the
sum of $l't'.<ft, together with the costs of suit.
This is the third suit of the same
sort decided . against Dalton, and he
now has $2800 Just collected from th<;
city for the last assessment rolls which
will al*o be the subject of a suit.

William S. Kennedy, who was shot
by William O. Stewart at the Brings
ranch, near Alvarado, has made a
statement of the affray to Deputy
Sheriff J. H. Reilly, who was sent to
the raucJi by Sheriff Bishop. Ken
nedy denies Stewart's claim that he
< Kennedy) tried to stab Stewart. The
wounded man insists that he was shot
while trying to BavÂ« his young wife
from being killed.
It is a queer tale that Kennedy nar
rates. He declares that he became
natisfied that his wife, Edith, had be
eome enamored of Stewart when the
three lived at San Bernardino. Fol
lowing along his narrative. Kennedy
told Deputy Reilly that he invited
Stewart after meeting him in San
Francisco to go to the Briggs place,
and there, in Kennedy's hearing, ask
the woman whom she loved best.
"We went to the ranch," said Ken
nedy, "and Stewart called my wife out.
while I hid in some brush. Stewart
asked my wife to go with him and she
refused, starting back into the house
and declaring she would stay with me.
"With that Stewart pulled out a
pistol and commenced shooting. I
jumped out to my wife's rescue and
that was when I was shot. Stewart
ran away as I fell to thÂ« ground."
Kennedy declared he had left San
Bernardino with his wife In order to
get away from Stewart, but the man
had followed them to San Francisco.
The wounded man is in a critical con
dition. Stewart teas brought back
from San Jose to-day.

Charles J. O'Connell, a landscape
painter and formerly a policeman, cut
his throat yesterday morning while
suffering from an attack of melan
cholia. He died within a few mo
ments.
He resided with his family at Al
Unden avenue. His brother, Geor^,
discovered the dead body In an out
house at 8:45 a. m. , after returning
from St. Ignatius Church.
The suicide retired from the police
force about twelve years ago. He was
a native of Ireland and 55 years old.
Two years ago while riding with Mar
tin Hogan In a buggy he was thrown
out and severely injured about the
head. His relatives informed Deputy
Coroner Meehan that ever since the
accident i O'Connell â¢ had been, acting
queerly at times and that he must
have been insane when he committed
the deed. ; .

Each year S. N. Wood &. Co. have been com
pelled to make extensive alterations to ac
commodate their fast'growlng trade. A few
days ago they leased the store .next to them
at 742 Market. This will give a great deal more
space for window display and provide sales
floor of considerable width and depth.- The
hat department will move into the new store,
thus having more room and better facilities.
The clothing department will be extended by
occupying the space formerly devoted to hats.
A. L. Peyser, the manager of S. N*. Wood &.
Co., when asked to what he attributed the
rapid growth of his firm, said:
"People will buy where they can get the
most for their money. Just as water seeks Its
level, the public will look for and patronize
that store that gives them full value for their
money and treats them with just considera
tion. And that's the whole etory in a nut
shell. I recall Emer6on's word*, for they
apply exactly to our establishment: "If a man
write a better book, preach a better sermon
or maXe a better mousetrap than his neigh
bor, though he build his house in the woods,
the world will make a beaten path to his
door." "
Five years ago when S. N. Wood & Co.
leased the butldlng formerly occupied by tbt
Golden Rule Bazaar many merchants du
biously shook their heads, thinking that the
step was too great. True, it was quite an
undertaking for a clothing firm. But later
developments proved conclusively that the
move was a. good one.

A month ago they reached Constan
tinople. At this time the yourrg ladies
were evidently in a nervous condition
and told stories of an attempt to kid
nap them near the Pyramids and of
their having been pursued across the
Galilee by Bandit Sheik. On the ar
rival of the three sisters in Vienna
their nervousness became more
marked. They dismissed or evaded
the courier and moved from hotel to
hotel, declaring that they were pur
sued by masked men. This went on
for several days until finally the un
fortunate sisters accosted a policeman

PARIS. June 8. â Friends of the
Misses Bondy of New York have re
ceived from Vienna news that the
young women are in a medical insti
tution in that city, having been taken
there for observation and treatment
while laboring under great excitement
or hysteria. '.
Six months ago the three young sis
ters left New York for Europe un
attended for a trin to the Mediterra
nean, Egypt and the Holy Land. Well
supplied with both letters of credit
and introduction, they sailed through
the Mediterranean and after visiting
the claFsic lands started for the Holy
I^ands and Syria, not, however, before
they had already had some unpleasant
experiences which- demonstrated that
traveling without a male escort or
chaperon in Eastern countries is
<juite different thing from what It
is in America.

Â¦ Mr. and Mrs. Williar will spend a few
weeks at Monterey, the Elysium of the
newly wed, and returning will take
up their abode in Mr. Williar's pictur
esque home on the hillside of Sausalito.
The season of 1904 has been replete
with smart weddings, but it remained
for the Huntsman-Willlar nuptials at
high noon yesterday to set the standard
of quiet elegance and delicacy of detail.
The ceremony uniting Miss Genevieve
Louise Huntsman and Harry R. Williar
Â¦was performed by the Rev. Dr. Clam
pett at the home of the bride, whtah
had been decorated with a profuslorr of
Hewers and ferns.
The bride, a statesque maid of superb
coloring, wore a white chiffon cloth
gown, hand made and 'trimmed with
rare old lace. She was attended by her
sister, Mrs. Kindleberger, as matron of
honor, and was given into the keeping
of the groom by her mother, Mrs.
George Huntsman.
Mr. Williar comes from an old Baltl
morean family of high repute, members
of which for a century have attained
distinction in professional and social
affairs.

three months ago I was attacked with that
terrible disease, locomotor ataxia. Power to
balance, stand, or even sit down, entirely lost.
Two best doctors of San Francisco and Hot
Springs. Ark., failed to help me. My stom-
ach, bowels and kidneys failed to work. I had |
catarrh of stomach, head and bladder as a re- j
suit of the disease; these troubles becamo,
chronic. At times my heart would beat like a j
drum, my eyes grew weak, my memory bad. ,
my nerves wrtcked. Three months ago I went
to Tuscan Springs. What happened since was J

stomach, bowels, bladder and kidneys In per- |
feet condition, eyesight perfect, nervousness >
gone, memory good. After three months' treat-
ment, .walked without a cane, with eyes closed
and up and down steep hills; am cured of this
dread disease, now walk with slight limp.
Have run stores in seventeen towns of the
State of Calitornia; am well known In down-
town districts of San Francisco; inquiry there
will prove truth of my helpless condition three
months ago. Further Information, address

er, City. The power of the President
to adjourn Congress is found in article
ill, section 3 of the constitution, which
reads as follows: "He (the President)
shall from time to time give to dbn
gress information of the state of the
Union and recommend to their con
sideration such measures as he shall
judge necessary and expedient; he
may, on extraordinary occasions, con
vene both houses or either of them,
and. in case of disagreement between
them, with respect to the time of ad
journment, he may. adjourn them to
such time as he shall think proper."

Vegetable Caterpillars.
The so-called vegetable caterpillar of
New Zealand is attracting much atten
tion at present, says Nature, of Lon
don. Fungoid parasites are common in
all parts of the world, but not gener
ally conspicuous enough to be noticed
by any but naturalists. Many of the
largest apd most remarkable moths of
'the Australian region are known to be
Infested .by various parasite fungi,
which convert the whole substance of
the caterpillar into a woody substance
and then sprout from it to the height
of several inches. The caterpillars
(which are usually about four inches
long when fully grown) live until they
are ready to asume the pupa state,
when they bury themselves in the
ground, .die,, and the fungus * sprouts
upward,' generally from the neck of
the caterpillar, sometimes acquiring a
length of- nearly a foot, and sprouting
up from the' ground above the cater
pillar. Very rarely two or even three
of these filaments may sprout from a
single caterpillar. The New Zealander's
names for . this plant-caterpillar are
hotete and acuhe. ' The natives eat the
plants, which, ' when fresn, have the
flavor of a out, and also use them,
when /burnt, as coloring matter for
their tattooing, rubbing the- powder
Into the wounds, in which state It has
a strong animal smell. .

THE great seal of the State of Oregon bears as its
sole legend "The Union." At the , time of the"a"d
, mission of Oregon as a State the bonds of the
Union had^een weakened in partisan controversy and
the paramount allegiance of millions of our countrymen
was unsettled. Oregpn, of her own act, set upon her
seal her great purpose to abide by the Union. Her voice
was uttered by her great Senator Baker and from her
position taken in that time of testing and trial she has
not swerved. Her election is'the first one of the year,
and she records her verdict for .the side represented by
Baker and Williams and stands steadfastly by the Re
publican party and President Roosevelt. " â¢
, The majority is sfgnificantly large.. Oregon is typical
of the -treat West. Within -her borders are nearly all
the industries that are peculiar to the country between
the -Pacific and the Missouri Valley. Her material inter
ests are common to all that territory, and the genius of
her people is kin to that' of the other twelve States in the
Western group. ".The political preference of her- people
is dictated by motives common to them all, and the re
sult of her election May be taken as an indication of
public sentiment in tnem all. The drift of the country
is Republican. Oregon votes first. Vermont and Maine
come next, and evidence of that drift will be as plain in
the result in the East as it is in the West.
The people see no reason for- a change now. No one
pretends that one party. must rule all the time, but the
people know. what the Republican party stands for. The
Democratic party'does not itself know what it stands
for or what it will do to get power. Even Democrats
who will .support their party again when it gets out. of
the insane asylum and ceases to see spooks and hide
from imaginary pursuers are' this year for Roosevelt and
the sane and vigorous Americanism for which he stands.
The votes of \uch Democrats ' have.. gone to ! swell the
Oregon majority and will be given to the same cause in
every- Northern ; State. .; :-. ... V .'Â¦ -. . ,-..

Don't Borrow Trouble.
Don't borrow trouble; It comes of itself.
Or if it does not, all the better for
you.
The care of to-morrow let's lay on the
shelf.
For Worry's an ugly and petulant
shrew.
Don't borrow trouble; the debt must be
paid.
And. ton, but the payment is heartache
*and wreck.
After all, when the cards have been
, shuttled and played.
Four aces, you'll find, were allowed to
your deck.
Don't borrow trouble: the care of to-day
Is easy to meet and easy to rout;
It's only the trouble from over the way
That leaves us the victims of terror
and doubt.
It's as easy to say "To-morrow 'twill
shine,"
As to moaningly mutter, "I'm sure it
will rain' r :
It's as , easy to smile as to weep and re
pine, ,'Â¦-',.;.. '
And the former, you'll find, is an in
tlnite gain.
Don't borrow trouble. We worry and fret.
Then find In theend that we've wor
ried for naught.
We build mlgbty hills In our pathway
to set. 'Â¦-Â¦Â¦
Then find the obstruction was child of
â¢ our thought.
You'll find on reflection that half of your
. care 1
Is a son of to-morrow that merely has
strayed.
The load of to-day Is sufficient to bear;
So don't borrow trouble â the debt
'must be paid.

Soldiers' Banker.
Tour Uncle Samuel la becoming
quite a banker for the American sol
diers. For the year ended June 30,
1903, there was deposited by soldiers
$1,883,014. and it is understood that
the amount for the fiscal year which
will end the last of this month will
be much larger. The enlisted men of
the United States are allowed to leave
their money in the hands of paymas
ters, taking a certificate for It, and the
Government pays the men Interest at
the rate of 4 per cent a year.
Many soldiers largely increase their
earnings by leaving their money on
.deposit as long as possible, and it
Is really a very good savings bank for
thÂ»m. The year before the Spanish
war the amount deposited was less
than one-third of that deposited in
1903. The enlisted strength of the
army has been increased since then,
and is now about doub'le what it was
before the Spanish war, but the
amount left on deposit is three times
as great as It was when the army was
smaller. Possibly service in the Orient
and the establishment of large post.*
have been conducive to saving on the
part of troops. At all events, officers
of the. War Department look upon the
increase as being an indication of
thriftiness upon the part of the Amer
ican soldiers. On several occasions at
tempts have been made in Congress to
allow officers of the army the same
privilege of leaving their money on
deposit, but such a bill has been de
feated every time. â Washington Post.
MAKK TWAIN PROCEEDED TO
FAN THE COP WITH THE CAB
BAGE Leaf.
stepped Into the California Market and.
going up to a vegetable stall, he pluck
ed a large leaf from a head of cabbage.
When he arrived at the place where
the big. fat policeman was fast asleep
the humorist proceeded to fan him
with the cabbage loaf. This amusing
scene soon attracted a crowd, which
Inside of ten minutes had swelled into
hundreds and California street was
blocked to traffic.
To add to the excitement, some one
had run to the old City Hall and In
formed Captain Douglass that there
had been a robbery at Wells, Fargo &
Co.'s, as the place was surrounded by
armed men. Douglass summoned every
available cop on "his force, which at the
time counted less than a dozen, and
rushed to the place designated. After
brushing the crowd to one side, he en
tered the gateway and there found
.Clemens whirling the cabbage leaf as
though nothing unusual had occurred
about him'. To say that Captain Doug
lass was mortified would be but a mild
expression. The drowsy cop lost , his
star and Clemens enjoyed the joke.
OFFICIAL criticism of the manner in which
dairies have been conducted in San Mateo, San
Francisco and Marin counties has awakened
the men who supply milk to this city to the necessity
for the exercise of a greater degree of cleanliness and
more care in their work. No sane head of a family
would buy impure milk, for by it the lives of himself and
of those who* are his natural care would be endangered.
At the best, if milk that is open to suspicion is used,
there is realization that health. is imperiled unnecessarily
and that a good article is not given in return for good
money.
Little children suffer most, for milk is a great part
of their food. When suspicion concerning the supply is
entertained less milk is consumed and less
is purchased.- The dairyman has therefore an interest
in having the public believe that he is dealing fairly.
The opposite opinion, generally held, is^ certain to in
terfere with dairy income.
There is full appreciation of what popular disfavor
means from the purely .business point of view. The
Marin County Tocsin makes some suggestions for the
benefit* of Marin County dairymen that are applicable
to the methods of all parties concerned as milk pro
ducers rind milk handlers.
The Tocsin combats every statement that matters are
not, on the whole, satisfactory among the dairies. of
Marin, but comes to the conclusion that it is wiser,
admitting that there are some cases for which no excuse
is possible, to have abuses attended to at home. Follow
ing this up, it is recommended that the Marin County
Board of. Supervisors adopt an ordinance regulating not
only the conduct of dairies engaged in the production
and shipping of milk for. the market, but of all others.
A certain standard of cleanliness, including a proper
supply of pure water for the stock, ought to be main
tained by ordinance. - To accomplish this a system of
inspection by some competent person is necessary.
The advantage of having it known, of all the counties
from which milk dromes into San Francisco as an article
of diet, that> proper precautions are enforced locally to
restrict marketing to the product that is fit to be con
sumed, is evident.
The Tocsin says it would be satisfactory to have it
understood that those parties who have brought re
proach upon the dairy industry must reform or go out
of business. The Board of Health of San Francisco
must maintain its^ interest in what is goii?g on relating
to the food supply originating in surrounding counties.
It is the duty of the members of this board to know
all that can- be learned concerning such supply. Vigil
ance ought never to be relaxed. The local authorities
in the neighboring counties have also a duty to perform.
The, sooner responsibility in the premises is understood
and the best possible methods are applied by all duly
qualified persons, the better it will be.
Still Ugly.
She Is a Woman's College sophomore
and was returning from a visit to New
Haven and transferring by street car
from the station in New York. She took
the only available seat in the car and
Juat opposite two young men. Sudden
ly there entered a ladylike, plainly
gowned girL "Why don't you offer her
your placer* said the first man, nudg
ing his companion. "She Is too ugly,"
responded the other In a low voice, but
the wind carried his remark to the
sophomore and she looked quickly to
see if it had reached the other girl.
Apparently It had not. That young
person was clinging to a strap In total
unconsciousness that she was a sub
ject for unfavorable criticism.
At the next corner a festive maiden,
elaborately costumed, entered, and the
first man bounded from his seat, which
the new-comer graciously accepted.
This ,lef t the second man at something;
of a disadvantage. He arose* also and
proffered his place to the girl who had
first entered the car. She surveyed*
him coldly.
"Thank you, no." she said In a clear
voice. "I am still Just as ugly aa I was
a few minutes ago." And the Baltimore
girl longed to embrace her and give
the college yell at the top of her youth
ful lungs.â Baltimore Sun.

Mark Twain's Joke.
Before Mark Twain made his name
famous in his first production of "The
Innocents Abroad" he was attached to
the staff of the old Alta California. It
Â¦was while thera that he perpetrated
one of his jokes, which at that time
had no more' significance than that of
an ordinary wag who enjoyed a little
fun at another's expense. But since
Mark has made his name known to the
reading world the joke will bear-re
peating.
It was one of those hot summer flays
that occasionally visit San Francisco
that Mr. Woodward, one of the propri
etors of the Alta, stepped into the ed
itorial room and there found Clemens
drawing on the end of a brier root pipe.
Woodward mopped his brow and when
he cooled down he began to deliver^
himself forcibly.
."I'm disgusted." said Woodward, "at
what I Ju/t saw on the street as I
passed by the carriage way leading
Into Wells, Fargo & Co.'s yard down at
California and Montgomery. Sitting
on a chicken coop, either drunk or
knocked out by the heat of the sun, is
a police offlcejyTast asleep."
"Let us take a look at the animal,"
said Clemens, getting up from his
desk and walking out. ' â¢
On his way down to the corner he
*-[-Â» HERE is much pessimistic talk about corporations
I in this country and their influence in legislation.
A It is all used as water on the wheels of the advo
cates o\f public ownership and operation of public utili
ties. It has been so often said that if the Government
does not assume ownership of the property of corpora
tions the corporations will own the Government that
shallow thinkers have come to assume it to be true.
It is time to call for a bill of particulars. What great
acts of legislation, Federal or State, have the corpora
tions secured that have been of permanent injury to the
people? The beginning of the kind of legislation com
plained of was the Credit Mobilier act of Congress. In
the original Pacific Railroad bill the (j Government re
tained a first mortgage lien upon the bonds which it
issued for the construction of the roads. The Credit Mo-
bilier secured legislation to convert that first lien into a
second mortgage, freeing the credit of the roads so that
they borrowed private 'capital secured by a Hen senior
to that of the Government.
When the roads were finished and the country was
getting the benefit of them this matter became a politi
cal issue. It drove. Oakes Ames, James Brook* and
Colfax out of public life. It entered into two Presiden
tial campaigns. It was threshed out on the stump and
in the press. But in the end the United States got back
every dollar of its loan with good interest. The second
mortgage bonds of the United States to the Central pa
cific road amounted to $25,000,000. To get the money
for construction that issue was sold by the corporation
for $18,000,000. But the Government was finally paid
$67,000,000 for its issue of $25,000,000, and the corpora
tion paid back $49,000,000 more than it got for the
bonds.
Did the people suffer any loss in that transaction?
The corporation lost heavily. Had it borrowed $18,000,
oco of private capital at the high rate of interest which it
paid the Government in 1863-4 it could have refunded
the debt at a much reduced rate. But it had to stand
the loss of $7,000,000 discount on the bonds and pay high
interest on $25,000,000. It was a losing investment for
the corporation but a good one for the treasury of the
United States. So it came about that in the first trans
action which politicians said proved that a corporation
owned the Government the Government made money
and the corporation lost.
Since then there has been no law of Congress that can
be' honestly said to have put the Government in the
power of corporations or worked to the disadvantage of
the people. On the other hand we have the interstate
commerce law, which is the broadest assertion of the
command of transportation corporations by the Govern
ment; and the Sherman anti-trust law, which extends
the same principle to all manufactures and commerce.
The courts have responded clearly and closely to the
spirit of such legislation, and we have the highest ju
dicial decisions in every case brought by direction of
President Roosevelt up to'.that which dissolves the rail
road merger. â¢ " - . â¢â¢ . _â¢ . .
So, as far as the Federal. Government is concerned,
there is a total lack of evidence that the corporations
control it. The pessimists and advocates of public
ownership are therefore driven back upon State legis
lation for proof of their jeremiads. Where is it?
Take our own State, for example. The great ques
tion that for years kept our politics in a stew was the
issue of taxation of the railroads. The Federal decision
in the San Mateo.case vitiated the assessment of rail
road property. Every clear-headed layman, to say
nothing of lawyers, knew the assessment was legally
void. The railroad did just as individuals'do when sub
ject to illegal assessment. It fought payment in the
courts and won. Then of its own motion it asked the
Legislature to give the necessary authority for a .retro
active assessment and openly introduced its own bill for
that purpose. Immediately this was denounced as a
railroad measure and a bill representing the anti-corpo
ration party was substituted for it and passed. When
the assessment was made under it the discovery resulted
that the road would have paid a much larger sum in
taxes under its own bill than it did under the anti-rail
road substitute. So that incident closed. But while it
was a vital issue it was put in evidence to prove that
the corporation owned the State. The fact is that our
Legislatures have shown more vigor than wisdom in
demonstrating their virtue and independence of corpo
rate control..
â¢> No- one questions that corporations have to resist mere
cinch bills and use what in England was called a Parlia
mentary fund in doing it. Under the English system,
however, has arisen a method superior to any so far
reached here. The great financiers, railroad builders
and owners and other representatives of corporations
are members of Parliament. If they want legislation
they must ask for it. frame it and give reasons for it. It
will be seen that this secures the greatest publicity for
such legislation, and its purpose. Here our methods "of
party management practically outlaw the presidents of
corporations from public. life. The only instance which
we recall in which one has been sent to Congress- was
the election of Governor Stanford to the Senate while
he was president 'of the Southern, Pacific.
It is quite worth while to give some study to the sug
gestion that this decree of outlawry be rescinded, and
that men at the head of great interests be permitted to
appear as legislators. It might take all the strain out
of the relation of corporations to the public. If Gould,
Harriman, Ripley or Rockefeller had to stand. up in the
House .or Senate and give reasons for or against legis
lation they would be brought face to face with the
people, instead of being suspected of. staying in the back
ground and inspiring a covert advocacy or opposition in
regard to proposed legislation.
direct line would be retained and many
were the congratulations he. and the
Marchioness received when a son was
born to them. The history of the peer
age in recent years furnishes only one
parallel case. The late Viscount Bol
ingbroke had no heir until he was 76
years of age. He married when he was
73 years old a daughter of Mr. Robert
Howard." and the . present Viscount,
who is now 8 years of age, was born
three years later.
In other respects his marriage to the
Canadian girl was a fortunate one for
the old Marquis. Besides other things
that be Jacked, she has a -head for
business and took the management of
his property into her own hands. He
was on the point of selling the bulk of
it to his tenants, through the agency
of the Irish Land Court, but she fixed
up the troubles over the rent with the
result that the property has been re
tained along with the ancient family
residence, Chichester jCastle. It is un
derstood that when the conventional
period of mourning expires the young
Marchioness will take 'a town house
and enter society, in which, with her
youth, vivacity, ambition good
looks, she is likely to make something
of a stir.
CORPORATIONS AND THE PUBLIC.
plan, as it would afford a high pressure to all parts of the
city." Continuing, the report declares that "the saltwater
could be used for domestic purposes and for otHeT muni
cipal uses, such as flushing sewers, etc. It would render
our city safe from such calamities as have befallen Chi
cago, Boston and Baltimore."
This confirmation of the eminently practical scheme of
the Board of Fire Commissioners should give weight to
the consideration of the project and lead the Supervi
sors to make investigations as to the practicability of its
details. Although instances are few in the records of the
Fire Department, of late years at least, where a lack of
an adequate water supply stultified the efforts of the fire
fighters, we cannot err too far on the side of safety in the
matter of such emergencies. To be foref ended against
possible disaster-'in this matter is to enjoy by just that
degree freedom from constant, uneasiness.
San Francisco is a city of wood. Probably there is
not another one of the large cities of the country so
generally constructed of wood as our- own. Balti
more, whose main streets of steel-framed buildings were
gutted by the great fire of February, would have seemed
not half so ready a victim for the flames as our own city,
built on the hills and wind swept every day in the year.
Now, after disaster has] visited Baltimore, plans are being
made there for the utilization of salt water after the same
manner as that recommended by the Board of Fire Com
missioners and seconded by the. Grand Jury here. We
of San Francisco, seemingly immune as we may be with
our excellent fire department and its* very efficient equip
ment, cannot tell the day nor the hour when a Balti
more or a Rochester disaster will strike on the city by
the Golden Gate. The establishment of an auxiliary salt
water reserve supply would be a very valuable means of
prevention. " . '

standing in the doorway of a house
opposite the saloon. They. could not

they were doing there and when taken
before Brzezinski he identified them

The supreme president was also em
powered to appoint a member of the
order whose duty it shall be to super
vise the laws and examine the by
laws of all the lodges to see that they
are In conformity with the constitu
tion and the general laws of the or
ganization.
In the evening there was an exem
plification of the initiatory work and
the installation of the new officers,
after which the supreme body ad
journed for one year.
It was decided that hereafter the
supreme president shall select a team
from the lodges for the purpose of
exemplifying the work of the order
before the supreme body immediately
before the installation of the new of
ficers.
The recommendations of Supreme
President Lillie A. Dygert that the of
ficers of the several lodges memorize
the initiatory work of the order; that
the floor work be presented in a uni
form manner, and that a fine be im
posed on members of the executive
board who fail to attend meetings or
go on official visits, except excused for
absence from the city or illness, were
adopted.
A proposition to prevent the use of
wines or liquors at any lodge meeting
or entertainment or function given in
tl*e name of the order was defeated.
A proposition to appoint a supreme
district deputy for all the lodges of
the order was defeated on the ground
that the lodges prefer a deputy for
each lodge.
At the afternoon session the action
of the previous day, which was to the
effect that none but a past president
of a subordinate lodge selected as a
delegate to the supreme body should
be eligible to the office of supreme
president, was rescinded and as in the
past any delegate will be eligible to the
office.

TIDE TURNS IX THE EIGHTH.
The affidavit denies, that the Cltlacns* Alli
ance I* hostile to the printers' union and de
clares that It is not- engaged In . printing or
selling any printed matter bearing, its label
The afQ'lavit says the purposes' above
enumerated are accomplished by means of the
boycott and strike. It adds that the union
la br-' is not used to advertise to the general
public products of superior skill but to pre
vert union men from buying superior goods
â¢mule by non-union houses. It declares the
label a disfigurement of- the printing of the
city nÂ»d county government. .
Secretary Files Affidavit Alleging
| Printers' Union Is Coercive.
In the proceedings begun by President French
of San Francisco Typographical Union No. 21
to prevent the Citizens' Alliance from using a
label alleged to be an infringement on the
label of the allied printing trades Secretary
W. E. Alexander of the Citizens' Alliance yes
terday filed with the County Clerk an affidavit
In which he averred that the Typographical
Union was organized to prevent the employ
ment of persons who were not members of the
union: to force the employment of Its members
an against other printers who are equally or
more competent; to compel employers to pay
higher > wages for the same inferior grade of
work; to destroy competition and to Injure tha
trade or business of persons who do not em
ploy union men. Â¦ -
ALLIANCE DISLIKES LABEL.

TWO STAKES WILL BE RUN
AT UNION PARK ON SUNDAY
CHICAGO, June 8. â Dr. Knudson,
â¢who operated . on Starter # Richard
Dwyer for appendicitis late "Saturday
night, reiterated his statement to-day
that Dwyer would certainly be able to
send the <horses away in the American
Derby at Washington Park on the 18 th
inst. Dwyer is making- much better
progress than had been anticipated
and not the slightest cause for alarm
is felt by Dr. Knudson or by the con
sulting physicians. It is only a ques
tion.'of a few days before Dwyer will
be out and around as strong as ever.
Dick Dwyer Recovers Rapidly.
Non-union freight handlers are rapidly load
ing the cars at the Townsend street depot.
The company has managed to secure 300 men
to take the places of the men who struck Sun
day night for an Increase of pay. When the
green hands went to work they could not keep
the sheds clear of goods coming In for ship
ment, but they are rapidly catching up and
the foremen in charge of the .gangs say the
new hands are improving daily.
Checking clerks were to have held a meet
ing at noon yesterday to finally settle the
question of whether they would Join the
strikers or remain at work. Without going
through the formality of a regular meeting
the decks decided to remain at work.
That action was a great drawback to the
freight handlers', for with the clerks out of the
sheds there would be better chances of the
company considering the demands of the men.
Disappointment was freely expressed by the
strikers when they learned of the action taken
by the clerks. No demonstrations have been
made by the men thus far, and they say there
will be none.
Non-Union Crews Are Clearing Sheds
While Strikers Are
Still Idle.

GRAVESEXD, June 8. â When Bar
dolph. a HO to 1 shot, beat out the
odds-on favorite. Ascension, in to
day's opening event, the betting ring
contained a jovial lot of bookmakers.
When a horne is Quoted at 50 to 1 it
moans he is practically unbacked and
when a favorite runs second at odds
on there is but little money to pay
out for a place. So the race practi
cally meant a clean sheet for nearly
all the layers.
There was. however, an awful reck
oning during the' remainder of the*
afternoon. Four heavily backed fa
vorites and a third choice landed, so,
notwithstanding the balance on the
ripht side of the ledger that Bar
dolph's victory gave the layers, the
bookmakers quit big losers on the day.
â¢â¢Joe" Yeager was a busy, man dur
ing the afternoon. He tried to make
a killing with Morris Kayraan's Or
t%>dox and backed him heavily from
six down to one. He failed, however.
N*cxt he plunged on Chrysitis in the
Clover stakes. Teager was making
l>ook to-day, but he laid "no price
against Chrypitis. While he was busy
laying against Czaraphine, Incantation
nnd - Simplicity, his commissioners
were betting on Pierre Lorillard's filly
around 3 to 1.
The victory of Chrysitis only netted
Teager about J 20.000 in bets, but his
book had practically a clean sheet and
it is estimated his total winning* were
clsse to $35,000. Summaries:

For a long time the contest at
Recreation Park yesterday was in
the gloomiest kind of doubt, and
no one had a line on what was due to
happen. Both teams were occasionally
tearing off a bingle and fielding for
fair. The Commuters just barely
squeezed a tally through in the sev
enth. Every one knew that was enough
to hold Henry and 'his for a while.
Score 3 to 1.
No fan could cdrnplalrr"6'f the variety
of ball passed out. Hitting there was
In plenty, with lots of bright fielding
and good pegging to bases by the
catchers. Buchanan was the steadier
pitcher with men on bases. He never
wavered, and when he did walk Irwin
in the ninth round the umpire gave him
a shade the worst of it. .
It started off like one of those ancient
battles of the bats where runs just
came streaming in. When two were
faded in the first round, Waldron land
ed one for two bags out to the score
board and Meany got a shot in the ribs.
Captain Irwin had a little hit stored
away which he produced at the right
moment, bringing Wally in.

BUCHANAX IS EFFECTIVE
One squint into the gymnasium this
morning would dissipate any idea one
might possess of , the champion's
lack of condition. For three or four
days, owing to Joe Kennedy's lame
wrist, he had not donned the gloves,
but this* morning -he -got the carpet
sweeper- out- and gave both Brother
Jack and Kennedy a thorough sweep
ing. The night before, while playing
on the opposing ball team, both of his
sparring partners incurred the dislike
of Jeff by their kicking tactics and he
promised them an enjoyable outing
next time out in the gym. He put each
through a fast four-round bout and a
horse clipper could not have performed
its work with more neatness nor dis
patch. *
Jack Jeffries had a square inch of
skin tissue removed from over one eye
and Kennedy looked as if he might
have got in range of the Japanese siege
guns planted on the heights command
ing the approaches to Port Arthur.
John Marcovich, a great friend of the
champion, dropped in from Calistoga
at this stage of the proceedings and
Jeff cut the balance of his work short.
After skipping the rope nearly 600
times, he quit and started for the
plunge. Before dinner a run down the
road completed his training for the
day.
After dinner Jeff officiated in a pig
sticking contest down at the pens. As
a result there were two porkers less
and to-morrow's guests will play
against roast pork and apple sauce,
straight, place and show.
The champion's face is beginning to
show the effects of his labor and he
says that now he must be careful and
not overdo things. After his fast
eight rounds with the gloves he scarce
ly took a long breath. Arrangements
have been made by the Jeffries camp
to leave for Oakland next Tuesday and
then the public can judge for itself
whether or not big Jim ia In condition.
HARBIN SPRINGS, June i.â If the
city sports fell for the yarn that Jim
Jeffries was in no shape and had
quietly asked for a postponement of
his contest with Jack Munroe, set
for the Mechanics' Pavilion on the
17th inst., it is the fault of no one at
this end of the line. Gullible people
can be found the wqrld over and may
be the ghost of P. T. Barnum has en
gaged in the boxing game, i
. When' 'Dick Adams, the Eddy-street
tavern-keeper and promoter, left here
last Saturday for San Francisco it
was to float his latest project. , Dick
claims to have discovered a well of
natural hot air in a huge cavern up
back of the training quarters and saw
millions in sight if it could be piped
to the city. Adams took along with
him several quicksilver flasks filled
with his discovery and his last words
as he climbed on the stage were,
'Watch me!" Dick gained some fame
as the composer of the war-time bal
lads, "When the Smoke Rolls Away,"
"Papa . Came Home Half Shot,"
"Matches, Matches, Matches," "Charge
of the Baltimore House Pipers," "Keep
My Gun in Good Repair," etc. Adams
was always a sensationalist and when
Jeff laughingly spoke of the. scare the
report ,had caused below he said, "I
wish I had a-hold of that sucker, what
a duckin' in the pond he'd get,"
Last summer, when the big fellow
was conditioning himself for his match
with Jim Corbett, there was a chance
then for a postponement. While fool
ing: with Brownie, the bear, the little
fellow sank his teeth in one of Jeff's
shins, and for a time it looked as If
there would be no. fight. This trip,
though, the bollermaker has not even
been run over by a freight train, kicked
in the face by a mule, lost an arm
monkeying around a buzz saw, blown
the shingles off his roof while out
shooting or been held up by female
road agents. In fact, he has enjoyed
a phenomenal run of luck.'.-
By F. E. Mulholland. 1

Jack Munroe proved . himself a
wicked puncher yesterday afternoon
in his work at the Beach Gymnasium
and it was the opinion of the spec
tators that if he exchanges blows with
Jeffries the people in Mechanics' Pa
vilion on the 17th instant will see the
greatest championship contest ever de
cided here.
Trainer in Chief Tim McGrath had
a string of aspiring heavy-weights to
be tried out yesterday and the man
from Butte went down the line in
short order. On the previous day
Manasso, the man who looks like
Jeffries, was given some love taps
which satisfied him that hewvas not
to be the successor of Je/TrTes. He
did not return yesterday. It is ex
pected there will be some more ab
sentees when the roll is called this
afternoon. Olin Sullivan was the first
victim. He started at a fast clip, but
Munroe soon took all the fight out of
him. Ivan McDonald was next on the
list and lasted less than a minute when
Tim McGrath called time. Joe Long
went down from a slight blow on the
body which showed Munroe's great
strength. Jack Sullivan. Andy Galla
gher and Frank Rafael also had on
the gloves. Gallagher and Rafael
took Munroe a fast boxing clip, but
he was ready for anything they had.
Munroe shows marked improvement
in his style of boxing. He blocks well,
hits a true blow and seems never off
his balance. . He 1b always set and can
hit from any position. He is built very
much on the lines of Young Corbett
and his muscles are not prominent.
He' never let up .in his gymnasium
work, going from the punching bag to
the swinging bag and then down the
line of the boxers without a rest. He
perspires freely and his flesh has a
ruddy, Kealthy appearance. His nose
is badly skinned as the result of some
rough work. As he will not. box for
three days before the tight, these slight
wounds will heal readily.
Owing to the crush of visitors at the
training quarters, an admission fee of
50 cents is exacted. This has not had
a deterring effect on the attendance,
the gymnasium being packed to its ca
pacity each afternoon.
Harry Pollock. Munroe's manager,
left Chicago on Tuesday night and is
due in. this city to-morrow.
CORBETT MAY FIGHT NELSON.
He Apparently Intends to Violate the
Agreement With Britt.
From present appearances Young Corbett
does not intend to abide by the agreement
signed by his manager binding him not to
appear in a fight until he meets Jimmy Britt.
Negotiations are pending for a fight here next
month with Battling Nel?on.
Corbett Is quoted as saying in >Â»"â¢Â¦*â¢ York
that he will meet any man of his weight
In the world regardless â¢ of any articles Britt
may hold. â¢ .
Manager Teddy Murphy has a number of
other offers for Nelson. The chief one Is
with Jimmy Briggs at Detroit on July 25.
Herrera and Yariger are to fight at Butts
on 'Miners' Union day. Louis Long and Jack
Clifford will nirnish the preliminary. Santry
and Long are to meet in Salt Lake early In
July.
Joe Boyle, matchmaker of the Dawson Ath
letic Association, has arranged a good match
for July 4. with the assistance of Harry Cor
bett. He has secure* Joe Mlllett and Billy
Woods to fight twenty rounds. The boxers
will leave at once for the north. Manager
Biddy Biehop will handle Woods.
Lincoln Club Boxing.
The monthly amateur boxing conteats of
the Lincoln Club will be held at Mechanics'
Pavilion Annex to-morrow night. Six bouts
are on the list. -Jo* Thomas and Bonar Wilt
will mix It In the main event. The remainder
of the programme is made up of well-known
amateur boxers. Milton Hares will do the
refereelng.
TEACHES MEN WHO HANG
JURIES A SHARP UESSON
Judge Gesford Marks His Displeasure
In a Way That Will Bring
Them to Time.
"Mr. Sheriff, you will see that those
men are properly cared for during the
night and if they are ready to bring
in a verdict by 10 o'clock to-morrow
morning I shall be prepared to receive
it. Counsel is excused till the same
time." . )
Judge Gesf ord Â¦ of Napa, who Is sit
ting in Judge Cook's court, gave these
orders at 5 o'clock yesterday after
noon, when it was made plain to him
that the jury In the case of John Pry
or. charged with burglary, was in
clined to disagree. The case was tried
yesterday and the Jury retired about
3 o'clock to consider the verdict, but
had not come to an agreement at 5
o'clock. Pryor is charged with enter
ing' the room of Ross Varnum at 18
Ellis street by means of the window
on the morning of January 1.
The Judge's action was caused by
the failure of the Jury in the case of
Henry Peterson, tried for burglary on
Tuesday, to agree, although it was
shown that the proprietor of the res
taurant entered had caught Peterson
in the act and had been knocked
senseless by a blow from an iron bar.
Under Sheriff Secures Witnesses.
Under Sheriff William D. Hynes re
turned yesterday from Quartz Valley,
â¢where he went to bring back Mr. and
Mrs. Rose Barnum to testify as wit
nesses in the trial of John Pryor, who
is charged with burglary in having en
tered the room of the couple at 18 Ellis
street last December. The presence
of the Bamums was necessary to in
sure Pryor's conviction. Hynes had
to travel over forty miles of rocky
roads' to serve the summons on the
Barnums and was glad to get back.
ROSE, THE GIANT ATHLETE,
WILL. COME TO STANFORD
World's Greatest Shotputtcr Says
There Are Too Many Knockers at
the University of Michigan.
ANN ARBOR. Mich., June 8. â The
University of Michigan will be minus
one star athleto when the time comes
for indoor training to begin next fall
if the statement made to-day by Ralph
Rose, the phenomenon of the year In
college athletics, is made good, as It
gives every reason of being.
"I will not come back to Michigan
next year." Rose stated. "There are
too many knockers around. I have
been talking with Stanford men who
were- at Chicago and I know what to
expect at the Palo Alto school. It Is
good enough .for me. If I come East
next year. It will be as a member of
the Stanford team."
Ambition in a pipe dream preceded by
a horrible nightmare and followed by
a rude awakening" when the pipe goes
out.

Much of Algoa's Cargo Damaged.
The schooner sighted off the Farallones Tues
day afternoon flying signals of distress proved
to be the Antelope, Captain Cuthrell, out three
days from Coquille River. The tug Sea Rover,
which waa dispatched to her assistance, towed
the disabled schooner Into port early yesterday
morning. Captain Cuthreil reports that June 6
at 10 a m.. when fifteen miles north of the
Farallones, the Antelope, was caught in a
northwest gale, during which she sprung a leak
over which the pumps had no control. She waa
loaded with lumber and could not sink. To add
to the woeÂ« of those on board, however the
rudder was carried away and the vessel be
came unmanageable. Part of her deck load
washed away and for thirty hours the crew'a
plight was perilous In the extreme. The loos
ened and ever dwindling deck, load increased
the difficulties of the situation, but all hands
stayed with the fight until help arrived. The
Rover took off the cold, tired and hungry aall
oxh,- housed and fed them and brought their
battered, craft safely to port, where It is now
tied up at the end of Green-street wharf.

The Coptic brought more passengers
than any c-f the smaller oriental liners
coming this way has carried in some
time. Homeward bound vessels will
be crowded, it Is said, for some time,
many business men coming home to es
cape the heat. When the Coptic left
Hongkong the demand for accommoda
tions was In excess of the available
space.
The Coptic's passengers Included the
following named:
Viscount T. Torii of Japan was a
passenger as far as Honolulu. He will
visit this country before returning to
Japan. Among the passengers who
arrived here yesterday was Mrs. Fred
Ringer of Nagasaki. Mrs. Ringer's
husband is a member of the firm cf
Holme, Ringer & Co. She is going to
Europe, making the trip for her health.
Captain Forteath and Captain R.
Kilbee-Stuart. both of the British
army, were passengers on th.e liner.
They .have been on duty in the Far
East and are returning to England on
leave of absence. There were several
missionaries among the Coptic's pas
sengers, which fact, according to the
sailors, accounted for the head winds
encountered coming up from Hono
lulu. |
The Occidental and Oriental Steam
ship Company's liner Co-ptk-, Captain
Armstrong, which arrived from the
Orient late Tuesday night, passed quar
antine after sunrise yesterday morn
ing and was alongside the Pacific Mail
wharf by 7 o'clock. The Coptic brought
about 80 cabin passengers, "27 steerage
and a large number of Japanese in
the Asiatic steerage. She brought 2201
tons of freight, which Included Japan
ese gold yen valued at $3,000,000. This
is said to- be the second largest single
shipment of gold ever sent out of
Yokohama. Her cargo included 5010
boxes of tea, 2467 bales of hemp. 641
bales of gunnies, 192 bales of raw silk.
143 bales silk soods and 100 bags of
coffee. Fine weather prevailed be
tween Hongkong and Honolulu. From
the island port to Fan Francisco the
Coptic bucked strong head winds.

many trips.

NEW YORK. June 8.â Mrs. Hannah
Ellas, who was arrested last night,
charged with extortion by John R.
Platt, was arraigned to-day and held in
550,000 bail for examination next Fri
day. Bail was not given and she was
committed to the Tombs prison.
When the prisoner was taken from
her cell to a cab, In which she was ta
ken to the Tombs Court, she was met
by a jeering crowd, which filled the
street around the police station. De
tectives, assisted by several patrol
men, cleared a passage and the car
riage continued to the court.
Justice Clarke, in the Supreme Court,
this afternoon issued a writ of habeas
corpus for Mrs. Elias, returnable to
morrow.

WE DID THE WORK.
The freight handlers' strike Is ov*r> â almost
before It was begun.
A more Ill-advised affair it would be hard
to conceive.
At a time when a large number of men arÂ«
walking the streets looking for work these
men saw fit to attempt to cinch the Southern
Pacific R. R. Co., and as a result now find
themselves out of Jobs while other and wiser
men hold their positions, and other hands will
receive the pay checks.
In the short space of three days wÂ« supplied
the R. Tt. Co. with 533 men. and could have
doubled the number If necessary.
In our "Labor Problem" we declarÂ«d our
conviction that the police of this city werÂ«
willing to do their duty if allowed to. and ths
events of the last few days have proven our
conviction to be correct. To Chief Wittman.
to Captain Splllane and the men under them
In the disturbed district, we wish to return
thanks for the protection which they afforded
OS while engaged in sending men there^ A
large number of strikers were gathered
around our offices and the railroad yards, yet
not a tingle case, of assault has occurred.
This irmaikable fact Is evidence enough of the
splendid protection, afforded by the police oC
San Francisco.
In conclusion we v wish to state that we har-
bcr no 1H feellnsr toward thÂ» unfortunate
strikers and, as we know no distinction be-
tween^ man and man, cur services in securing
other positions for them are at their disposal,
should they see fit to avail themselves of them.
MURRAY & READY,
t>34 and 636 Clay St.

1 .....DIREGTIONS......
TO OPEN CAN ON TOP. Force point of bar into center of top of. unsealed or smooth end of 'can.
Bend over until the bar is parallel with the top of can. Set point of lower blade on top of can at whatever
distance from edge the opening js desired. Press down handle until blade penetrates the tin. Then pull
gently toward you, holding the c__. tightly in the other hand. (To get best result hold can by top).
TO OPEN CAN FROM SIDE. Remove label from can. Force point into exact center of top. Bend
over until bar rests on edge of can. Set point of cutting blade against side of can. always at left of side
seam, with lever perpendicular^ Press down on lever, which will force side blade into can. Hold can tightly
by top with other hand and cut"s_n___as when cutting top. In cutting top off always let bottom of .bar rest
and run evenly around edge of can to insure even, regular cut. If the side blade comes out while cutting, or
cuts in too far and stops, it is because you have not inserted point of bar in exact center of can. In this case
throw up lever, readjust blade and continue cutting until blade reaches opposite side of seam, when top can
be lifted or broken off altogether. Do not bo afraid to grasp can firmly by the top.

You cannot cut yourself.

COMMERCIAL NEWS
The graduation exercises of the Irv
ing M. Scott Grammar School were
held yesterday in the school building,
Tennessee street, near Twenty-second.
Under the direction of Miss M. M.
Murphy and before an audience of
schoolmates, relatives and friends the
exercises, consisting of < recitations,
songs and addresses, consumed the
afternoon. The feature of the after
noon was the singing of Misa May
Daly. Following the exercises came
the awarding of the diplomas by Dep
uty Superintendent Suzzalo. The Den
man medal was awarded to Miss Mary
Dradyand the Bridge medal to Carl
Anderson. *
' The following are the graduates:
Pearl Brainerd, Mary Burns, Mary
Conlan. Mary Drady. Maud Elliott,
Florence Keane. Nellie Keating. May
Leary, Martha Lucy, Grace Mackin
tosh. Edith McKay. Agnes Reynolds,
Gertrude Rollins, Irene Timby. Eva
Turner, Anna von Husen. Carl Ander
son, Clyde Berglund; Daniel Keefe,
Paul Krug, John McCabe and "William
Ruffler.
Irvine Scott School.
With the spread of education dead
languages and foreign tongues are no
longer mysteries soluble only by the
cultured few. The famous Eton boy
who rendered "splendid mendax"' as
"lying Jn state" is but a pioneer. Quite
recently a pupil teacher, fairly well ad
vanced in her teens, who at a teachers'
center is still learning to read French
with a furious Cockney accent, and to
decipher it, was taken to lunch at a
French restaurant in Soho. Scanning
the menu with a show of Interest which
a healthy appetite alone could hardly
have excited, she was overheard to
murmur: "Canard sauvage aux pctits
pols" ("Theduck Is savage at the few
peas").â London Daily Chronicle.

A Duke's â¢ Treasures.
In illustration of the lavishness with
which Chatsworth House is endowed
with art treasures and of the distant
element which Is supposed to be a fea
ture of the Duke of Devonshire's mind
an amusing story went the round of
the French press at the time of the
last Paris exhibition. The 'Duke, it was
said, was strolling through the loan
section of the English exhibits with a
friend and . stopped to look with ad
miration at a porphyry table of match
less beauty. He examined it long with
the eye of a connoisseur and j at last
exclaimed: "I wonder who is the owner
of such a splendid specimen of work
manship. I almost feel inclined to envy
him." His companion, who had con
culted the catalogue,' handed it to him
with a smile. It contained the infor
mation that the table came from
Chatsworth House and was lent by the
Duke of Devonshire.â- London Chron-Â»

WHITE SECURES CHILD.â M. J. White.
secretary of the Society for the Prevention of
Cruelty to Children, yesterday obtained from
Judge Murasky a writ of habeas corpus by
which he will reobtaln possession of Violet
Lundborg. He was appointed her guardian and
placed her In the orphan asylum, from which
institution she was taken by Mrs. Kate
Thompson, a friend of the child's mother.
The mother's petition to secure custody of the
I girl was denied by the Supreme Court.
j HISTORY OF THE BOGOTA.â A regular
meeting of the San Francisco section of the
| Navy League of the United States was held
last evening at the rooms of the Chamber of
Commerce, 307 Sansome street. The meeting
was presided over by Edward Mills Adams;
the chairman of the section. An extremely.ln
tt-reetlng informal talk was given by A. H.
Dutton, formerly lieutenant In the United
States navy, on the trip of the Colombian
gunboat Bogota, fitted out at San Francisco,'
to meet the Insurgent boat Padilla.

NEWSBOY KILLEDâ OAKLAND. JunÂ« 8.â
Sam Rhodes, : a newsboy, was Killed to-night
by a Berkeley local train. The body was
found on the Southern Pacinc Company's
tracks on Cedar street, between Ninth and
Chase streets. It is supposed the boy fell off
the train and was crushed by the wheels. The
Coroner took charge of the remains. Rhodes'
mother. Mrs. Eleanor Rhodes, resides ln Los
Angeles.

WHEATâ The foreign markets continued

acres. The tone of the market Is heavy, with
a dragging tendency.
The local market is firm as to cash grain,
but futures are lower again.
